## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: materials science (solid mechanics) — thermo-mechanical constitutive modeling
**Analysis**: The variables are stress (sigma), strain (epsilon), and temperature (T), which is the standard setting for constitutive laws describing elastic/anelastic/plastic response and temperature-dependent stiffness/softening (thermoelasticity and high-temperature flow stress models).

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Linear thermoelastic (Hooke + thermal strain): sigma = c0 * (epsilon - c1 * (T - c2)) (elastic stress with thermal expansion offset)
- Temperature-dependent modulus (linear): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon (elastic modulus varies approximately linearly with temperature over a range)
- Temperature-dependent modulus (exponential): sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * T) * epsilon (stiffness changes multiplicatively with temperature; c1 often negative for softening)
- Nonlinear elasticity (polynomial): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon + c2 * epsilon**2 (mild geometric/material nonlinearity with temperature-dependent tangent modulus)
- Hyperelastic-like exponential stiffening: sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * epsilon) * exp(c2 * T) (rubberlike or lattice-based stiffening plus thermal scaling)
- Ramberg–Osgood-style explicit stress (power hardening with thermal scaling): sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * exp(c2 * T) (work hardening with temperature-dependent strength)
- Additive hardening + thermal softening: sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2) * exp(c3 * T) (baseline + strain hardening, all softened/strengthened by T)
- Rational thermal softening of flow stress: sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2) / (T + c3) (strength decreases roughly like 1/T at high T)
- Thermoelastic + nonlinear stiffening (combined): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * (epsilon - c2 * (T - c3)) + c4 * epsilon**2 (thermal strain shift plus nonlinear response)
- Thermally activated softening with logarithmic T: sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2) + c3 * log(T + c4) (strength changes approximately with log temperature over a regime)
- Near-melting softening factor (using reference temperature): sigma = (c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2) * (c3 - T)**c4 (strength collapses as T approaches a critical value c3)
- Two-mechanism mixture (sum of exponentials): sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * epsilon) * exp(c2 * T) + c3 * epsilon**c4 (coexisting mechanisms: e.g., elastic lattice + plastic hardening)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Thermal expansion often appears as a strain shift: use (epsilon - c * (T - c0)) so stress can be near zero at nonzero epsilon when T changes.
- If stiffness decreases with temperature, represent it as a multiplicative modulus factor E(T) multiplying epsilon, commonly E(T) = c0 + c1 * T or E(T) = c0 * exp(c1 * T) with c1 < 0.
- Plastic/flow stress typically factorizes into a strain-hardening part and a thermal-softening part: sigma(epsilon,T) ≈ g(epsilon) * h(T).
- Hardening behavior is frequently a power law in strain: g(epsilon) = c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2, which captures rising tangent modulus at small-to-moderate plastic strains.
- When strength drops strongly at high temperature, rational forms like 1/(T + c) or critical-point factors like (c - T)**p can capture rapid softening near a transition (e.g., approaching melting).
- Logarithmic temperature dependence (log(T + c)) can approximate thermally activated mechanisms over limited ranges where changes are gradual per temperature decade.
- Combining additive terms in sigma can represent parallel load-bearing mechanisms (e.g., elastic lattice term plus strain-hardening term) that respond differently to T.
- Exponential-in-strain forms exp(c * epsilon) are useful for materials showing rapid stiffening at larger strain (some polymers/hyperelastic regimes) and can be modulated by temperature via an additional exp(c * T) factor.

I need to analyze each candidate against the Ground Truth (GT):

**GT:** `61.56*epsilon^0.4776*exp(-55.88/(8.314*T)) + epsilon^3 * 7.641*(T - 344.46)`

Let me expand the GT:
`61.56*epsilon^0.4776*exp(-6.722/T) + 7.641*T*epsilon^3 - 2632.6*epsilon^3`

The GT has these basis functions:
1. `epsilon^0.4776 * exp(-6.722/T)` — a fractional power of epsilon times a Boltzmann-like exponential in T
2. `T * epsilon^3` — product of T and epsilon cubed
3. `epsilon^3` — epsilon cubed

The key challenge: the GT contains `exp(-c/T)`, which is a very specific function of T. No polynomial, logarithmic, trigonometric, or simple power of T can exactly replicate `exp(-c/T)` for all T.

Let me check which candidates have the right structural elements.

**Critical observation:** None of the candidates contain `exp(-c/T)` or `exp(c/T)`. Let me look for candidates that might encode this differently.

Looking through all candidates, the T-dependence appears as:
- `T*c0*(c1+epsilon)^3` (many candidates) — this gives T times a polynomial in epsilon
- `exp(T*c)` (candidates 20, 32, 38) — this is exp(c*T), NOT exp(c/T)
- `log(T*c+1)` (candidates 14, 34)
- `1/T^2`, `1/T^(1/3)`, `sqrt(T)`, `T^(1/3)`, `T`, `1/T`
- Some candidates have no T dependence at all

**The GT requires `exp(-c/T)` dependence.** This is fundamentally different from:
- `exp(c*T)` — different function
- `log(c*T+1)` — different function  
- `T^n` for any n — different function
- Any polynomial/rational function of T — different function

None of the candidates contain `exp(c/T)` or any equivalent form. The function `exp(-c/T)` cannot be exactly represented by any of the basis functions present in the candidates (polynomials in T, exp(c*T), log(T), powers of T, etc.).

Even for candidates with `T*(c1+epsilon)^3` which can produce the `T*epsilon^3` term, they still need `exp(-c/T)` for the first term, which none provide.

Let me verify there's no clever algebraic trick I'm missing. The term `epsilon^0.4776 * exp(-6.722/T)` requires both a fractional power of epsilon AND an exponential decay in 1/T. Since exp(-6.722/T) is not a constant (it varies with T from 0 to 1), and no candidate has this T-dependence, no candidate can match.

For candidates with no T-dependence at all (like 4, 7, 8, 21-28, 37, 46-48, 50): they can't match because GT depends on T.

For candidates with T-dependence through `T*epsilon^3` type terms: they can capture the second part of GT but not the `exp(-c/T)` part.

Going through each candidate systematically, none can produce `exp(-c/T)` dependence on T, which is essential in the GT.
